Mahjong advisor product spec Document · MD Simplify everything.Create a VERY simple MVP logic system for this mahjong app.Only use:- suit concentration- pairs- sequences- honors- jokersLimit to:- 10 hand types maximumNo complex scoring.Just rank top 3 recommendations based on strongest patterns.Give:1. simple logic rules2. 3 example inputs + outputs3. explanation of why each recommendation is chosen Apr 1 Here's the simplified logic system — clean, practical, and buildable. The 5 Pattern Signals Before any hand-matching, the app reads 5 things from the player's 13 tiles: 1. Suit concentration — which suit has the most tiles, and how many 2. Pairs — any tile appearing exactly twice 3. Sequences — three or more consecutive numbers in the same suit (e.g., 3-4-5 Bam) 4. Honors — winds and dragons in hand, especially duplicates 5. Jokers — wild cards; each one covers any missing tile in a group The 10 Hand Types # Name What it needs Joker-friendly? Beginner? H1 Even Run Even numbers (2,4,6,8) in one suit, in groups Yes Yes H2 Odd Run Odd numbers (1,3,5,7,9) in one suit, in groups Yes Yes H3 Single Suit Stack 3–4 of the same number, repeated across one suit Yes Yes H4 Consecutive Pairs Pairs of neighboring numbers in one suit (e.g., 44-55-66) Yes Moderate H5 Same Number Across Suits Same number appears in 2 or 3 different suits Yes Moderate H6 Honor Anchor 2+ pairs of winds or dragons anchoring a hand Partial No H7 Dragon Run Three or four dragons (G, R, W) in groups Partial No H8 Mixed Pair Hand 5+ pairs across suits and/or honors Yes Yes H9 Sequence Hand Two or more 3-tile runs in one suit (e.g., 1-2-3, 4-5-6) No Moderate H10 Joker Bridge Flexible hand requiring 2+ jokers to complete gaps Yes (required) Yes Simple Ranking Rules The app checks each of the 10 hand types and scores them on 4 questions only: How many tiles already fit this hand? (0–13, higher = better) Does the player have the right pairs? (+2 points per matching pair) How many tiles are still missing? (subtract 1 point per missing tile) Does a joker help? (+2 per joker if the hand is joker-compatible) Top 3 scores win. Ties broken by fewest missing tiles. One disqualifier: if a hand needs something the player has zero of (e.g., H6 needs honor pairs but player has no honors and no jokers) → skip it entirely. Example 1: The Bam-Heavy Hand Input tiles: BAM 2, BAM 2, BAM 4, BAM 4, BAM 6, BAM 6, BAM 8, DOT 3, CRK 5, WIND-E, WIND-E, JOKER, JOKER What the app sees: Suit concentration: 6 Bams, all even numbers Pairs: BAM-2, BAM-4, BAM-6, WIND-E (four pairs) Sequences: none (no consecutive numbers) Honors: 2 East Winds (one pair) Jokers: 2 Scoring: Hand Tiles fit Pairs match Missing Joker boost Total H1 Even Run 6 +6 (3 pairs) -2 +4 14 H4 Consecutive Pairs 6 +6 (3 pairs) -3 +4 13 H8 Mixed Pair Hand 8 +8 (4 pairs) -4 +4 16 ← winner Top 3 Recommendations: 🥇 H8 — Mixed Pair Hand (score: 16) You already have 4 pairs. This hand type is built around pairs across tiles and honors. Your jokers cover two remaining gaps. 🥈 H1 — Even Run (score: 14) All 6 of your Bams are even numbers. This is a natural fit. Discard the Dot and Crack — they're dead weight here. 🥉 H4 — Consecutive Pairs (score: 13) Your BAM-2, BAM-4, BAM-6 pairs sit next to each other numerically. Many hands on the card use pairs of neighboring numbers. You need BAM-8 as a pair to complete this path — you already have one. Example 2: The Scattered Hand (Hard Case) Input tiles: BAM 1, BAM 5, DOT 2, DOT 7, DOT 9, CRK 3, CRK 3, CRK 6, WIND-N, WIND-N, DRG-G, DRG-G, JOKER What the app sees: Suit concentration: 3 Bams, 3 Dots, 3 Cracks (no dominant suit) Pairs: CRK-3, WIND-N, DRG-G (three pairs — two honor pairs) Sequences: none Honors: North Wind (pair), Green Dragon (pair) — two honor pairs Jokers: 1 Scoring: Hand Tiles fit Pairs match Missing Joker boost Total H6 Honor Anchor 4 +4 (2 honor pairs) -4 +2 6 H8 Mixed Pair Hand 6 +6 (3 pairs) -6 +2 8 ← winner H7 Dragon Run 2 +2 -6 +2 0 → disqualified (too far) Top 3 Recommendations: 🥇 H8 — Mixed Pair Hand (score: 8) You have 3 pairs already — Cracks, North Wind, Green Dragon. This is your clearest path even with a scattered hand. Draw for more pairs wherever you can. 🥈 H6 — Honor Anchor (score: 6) Two honor pairs (winds + dragons) is a strong signal. Some hands use honors as anchors alongside a suited group. Keep both honor pairs — don't break them. 🥉 H10 — Joker Bridge (score: 4) With a scattered hand and 1 joker, a flexible joker-dependent hand gives you an escape route if your pairs don't develop. Low priority unless you pick up a second joker. Note: This is a genuinely hard hand. The app also tells the user: "No dominant suit detected — flexibility is your best asset right now." Example 3: The Strong Sequence Hand Input tiles: DOT 1, DOT 2, DOT 3, DOT 4, DOT 5, DOT 6, DOT 7, DOT 8, DOT 9, CRK 5, CRK 5, JOKER, JOKER What the app sees: Suit concentration: 9 Dots — full 1–9 run Pairs: CRK-5 (one pair) Sequences: DOT 1-2-3, DOT 4-5-6, DOT 7-8-9 (three complete runs) Honors: none Jokers: 2 Scoring: Hand Tiles fit Pairs match Missing Joker boost Total H9 Sequence Hand 9 +0 -1 0 8 H2 Odd Run 5 +0 -3 +4 6 H3 Single Suit Stack 9 +0 -2 +4 11 ← winner Top 3 Recommendations: 🥇 H3 — Single Suit Stack (score: 11) You have all 9 Dot tiles. This gives maximum flexibility within single-suit hands that require groups of the same number (e.g., three 3-Dots, three 7-Dots). Your jokers fill any needed group to 3 or 4. This is the strongest single path. 🥈 H9 — Sequence Hand (score: 8) 1-2-3, 4-5-6, 7-8-9 in Dots is already complete. Some hand patterns require exactly these three runs. You may need one more specific tile or a pair — your Crack pair plus jokers can cover this. 🥉 H2 — Odd Run (score: 6) You have DOT 1, 3, 5, 7, 9 naturally within your run. If a specific hand needs odd-only Dots in groups, you're halfway there. Lower priority than H3 but worth watching. Why Each Recommendation is Chosen — The Plain Logic Suit concentration drives the first filter. If you have 6+ tiles in one suit, the app immediately favors single-suit hands (H1, H2, H3, H4, H9). If no suit dominates, the app leans toward pairs and honors (H6, H7, H8). Pairs are the second signal. Pairs are useful in almost every hand type. Three or more pairs point strongly toward H8. Two honor pairs point toward H6. Pairs of consecutive numbers in the same suit point toward H4. Sequences narrow the field fast. If you have 3+ consecutive numbers in one suit, H9 jumps up. If you have all even or all odd numbers, H1 or H2 activates. Jokers act as multipliers, not hand-definers. They don't point to a specific hand — they boost the score of whichever hand already fits best. Two jokers make almost any hand more viable. H10 (Joker Bridge) is only recommended when nothing else has strong signals. Honors are specialty signals. Two pairs of honors = H6 becomes relevant. Otherwise honors are passive and don't influence the top recommendations unless duplicated. Missing tiles are the honest penalty. A hand that looks like a great match but needs 7 more specific tiles is penalized heavily. The app surfaces paths you can actually reach, not ideal hands that require a complete rebuild. This entire system can be built as a single JavaScript file with a JSON hand library. No database, no AI API, no backend. Just clean pattern-matching logic your users will understand in 30 seconds. Apr 1 Here's the refined system with concealed-hand difficulty baked in — still simple enough to build, but now honest about real-world achievability. What's Being Added and Why In live American mahjong, some hands must be won without ever claiming a discard (concealed). Others can be built openly by picking up discards. A hand that looks like a perfect tile match on paper can be extremely hard to complete if the player can never take from the table. The system needs to reflect this reality so recommendations don't mislead players into chasing hands they can't realistically win. Two new fields are added to every hand type. One penalty system adjusts the final score. The user sees a plain note when it matters. Two New Fields Added to Every Hand Type exposure_type — can the player claim discards to build this hand? exposed — player can openly claim discards; hand can be seen by others at the table concealed — player must win only from the wall (their own draws); no claiming discards either — both paths exist for this hand type completion_difficulty — how hard is this hand to complete in live play, independent of tile fit? easy — straightforward structure, flexible tile requirements, commonly completed medium — requires some specific tiles or pairs; achievable but needs focus hard — very specific tile requirements, tight structure, or must be concealed Updated 10 Hand Types # Name Exposure Type Completion Difficulty Notes H1 Even Run either easy Flexible structure; can be built openly H2 Odd Run either easy Same as H1 with odd numbers H3 Single Suit Stack either easy Very common; discards help a lot H4 Consecutive Pairs either medium Needs specific pairs; exposure helps H5 Same Number Across Suits exposed medium Relies on claiming discards by definition H6 Honor Anchor concealed hard Honor-heavy hands are typically concealed and tile-specific H7 Dragon Run concealed hard Requires exact dragon groupings; no discard help H8 Mixed Pair Hand either medium Pairs can come from wall or discards depending on variant H9 Sequence Hand concealed medium Sequential runs are often concealed on the card H10 Joker Bridge either easy Jokers reduce difficulty regardless of exposure type The Concealed-Hand Penalty System Base Penalty by Exposure Type and Difficulty Exposure Type Completion Difficulty Penalty Applied to Score exposed easy 0 exposed medium 0 either easy 0 either medium −2 either hard −3 concealed easy −2 concealed medium −4 concealed hard −6 The penalty is subtracted from the raw score after all positive signals are counted. It represents the real-world friction of building a hand without ever touching a discard. Why the Penalty Exists A concealed hand removes the player's most powerful tool: claiming discards. In a typical game, a player may draw from the wall only 10–14 times. If a hand needs 4 specific tiles and the player can never claim them off the table, the probability of completion drops sharply. The penalty makes the scoring system honest about this, even if the tile fit looks excellent on paper. When the Penalty Is Reduced The concealed penalty is not fixed. Three conditions reduce it because they partially offset the difficulty of building without discards. Reduction 1: Strong Starting Tiles Condition: Player already holds 8 or more tiles that directly support the concealed hand. Reduction: −3 off the penalty (minimum penalty of 0). Reasoning: If the player is already most of the way there, the number of wall draws needed is small. The concealment constraint matters much less when you only need 1 or 2 more tiles. Reduction 2: Jokers in Hand Condition: Player holds 1 joker. Reduction: −2 off the penalty. Condition: Player holds 2 or more jokers. Reduction: −4 off the penalty (minimum penalty of 0). Reasoning: Each joker is effectively a free draw. A joker substitutes for a tile you would otherwise need to find from the wall. Two jokers in a concealed hand dramatically change the odds. Reduction 3: Hand Flexibility Condition: The hand type has a completion_difficulty of easy even though it is concealed. Reduction: −2 off the penalty automatically (already reflected in base penalty table above). Condition: The hand type is either with multiple valid tile combinations. Reduction: −1 off the penalty. Reasoning: A flexible hand gives the player more ways to complete it from the wall. Rigid hands with exact tile requirements punish the player more severely when they cannot claim discards. Combined Reductions — Example A concealed/hard hand starts with a penalty of −6. Player has 9 matching tiles (−3) and 2 jokers (−4). Net penalty: −6 + 3 + 4 = penalty reduced to 0. Hand is scored without any penalty. This correctly reflects a player who is nearly done and holding jokers — concealment is almost irrelevant at that point. 3 Examples: Good Tile Fit, Downgraded by Difficulty Example A: Honor Anchor Looks Tempting, Gets Penalized Input tiles: WIND-E, WIND-E, WIND-E, DRG-G, DRG-G, DRG-G, BAM-5, BAM-5, DOT-3, CRK-7, CRK-7, JOKER, JOKER Raw tile fit score for H6 (Honor Anchor): 14 6 honor tiles matching, two strong triplets, 2 jokers Penalty applied: H6 is concealed / hard → base penalty −6 Player has 2 jokers → reduction +4 Player has 6 matching tiles, not yet 8 → no strong-start reduction Net penalty: −6 + 4 = −2 Adjusted score: 12 Why it's still recommended but flagged: The tile fit is exceptional — triple Winds and triple Dragons are rare and powerful. The jokers absorb most of the concealed penalty. This hand stays in the top 3 but the user is told clearly that it requires wall draws only. Recommendation note shown to user: "This path fits your tiles well. However, this is a concealed hand — you cannot claim any discards. You must draw everything you still need from the wall. Your two jokers help significantly, but plan accordingly." Example B: Sequence Hand Gets Outranked Despite Perfect Runs Input tiles: DOT-3, DOT-4, DOT-5, DOT-6, DOT-7, DOT-8, DOT-3, DOT-5, CRK-2, BAM-9, WIND-S, WIND-S, JOKER Raw tile fit score for H9 (Sequence Hand): 13 Near-complete DOT sequences; strong suit concentration Raw tile fit score for H3 (Single Suit Stack): 11 Penalty applied to H9: H9 is concealed / medium → base penalty −4 Player has 1 joker → reduction +2 Player has 8 Dot tiles → strong start reduction +3 Net penalty: −4 + 2 + 3 = +1 → penalty eliminated, no deduction Penalty applied to H3: H3 is either / easy → base penalty 0 Adjusted scores: H9 = 13, H3 = 11 Outcome: H9 wins here because the player already has 8 matching tiles. The concealment penalty is fully erased by strong starting tiles and a joker. This is the right call — the player barely needs the wall. Recommendation note shown to user: "This is normally a concealed hand, meaning no discards. But you're already holding 8 of your needed tiles — you're close enough that this constraint barely matters. Focus your draws on completing the Dot sequences." Example C: Dragon Run Looks Exciting, Gets Bumped to Third Input tiles: DRG-G, DRG-G, DRG-R, DRG-W, BAM-4, BAM-4, DOT-6, DOT-6, DOT-8, CRK-3, WIND-N, WIND-N, JOKER Raw tile fit score for H7 (Dragon Run): 10 4 dragon tiles present, joker could help Raw tile fit score for H8 (Mixed Pair Hand): 11 4 pairs already (BAM-4, DOT-6, WIND-N, plus potential DRG-G) Penalty applied to H7: H7 is concealed / hard → base penalty −6 Player has 1 joker → reduction +2 Player has only 4 matching tiles, not yet 8 → no strong-start reduction Net penalty: −6 + 2 = −4 Adjusted score for H7: 10 − 4 = 6 Adjusted score for H8: 11 − 0 = 11 Outcome: H7 drops from first to third despite the dragon excitement. H8 wins because it requires no concealment and the player already has strong pairs across multiple tiles. Recommendation note shown to user: "You have 4 dragons, which is tempting — but this hand must be completed without claiming any discards. With only 1 joker and 4 dragons needed in specific groupings, the odds of finishing from the wall alone are low. This is worth watching but not worth committing to early." Rewritten Recommendation Output Format Every recommendation card now shows one of three plain-English exposure notes depending on the situation. When a hand is exposed or either, no penalty, no note needed: The card shows nothing about exposure. Clean and simple. When a hand is concealed but penalty is mostly erased: "Concealed hand — but you're in good shape." You need wall draws only (no claiming discards). Because you're already holding [X] of your key tiles [and/or have jokers], this is still a realistic path. When a hand is concealed and the penalty is significant: "Concealed hand — harder than it looks." This hand cannot use any discards. You would need to draw [N] more specific tiles entirely from the wall. In a typical game, this is a difficult path to complete. Consider this a secondary option unless your draws go well early. When a hand is concealed and disqualified entirely (penalty exceeds tile fit score): The hand is removed from recommendations silently. The top 3 shown are always realistic options, not aspirational ones. How This Changes the Overall Flow The logic sequence is now: Read the 5 pattern signals (suit concentration, pairs, sequences, honors, jokers) — same as before Score raw tile fit for all 10 hand types — same as before Apply concealed-hand penalty based on exposure type and completion difficulty Apply penalty reductions for strong starting tiles, jokers, and flexibility Disqualify any hand where adjusted score falls below 3 Rank remaining hands by adjusted score Generate recommendation output, including exposure note when relevant The user always sees recommendations they can actually pursue. The system is honest without being discouraging — it tells the player when a hand is harder and why, then points them toward the most achievable path given what they actually hold. can you call honors winds? Also are you aware of the differet sections of the card? 2026? Evens, Any like numbers etc.... Apr 1 Great questions — let me address both directly. Terminology Fix: Honors → Winds Yes, absolutely. In American mahjong, Winds is the correct and familiar term for East, South, West, North tiles. "Honors" is borrowed from other mahjong variants and would confuse American players. Updated everywhere going forward: WIND_E, WIND_S, WIND_W, WIND_N stays as the internal ID convention The UI label and all player-facing text now says Winds, not Honors Dragons remain Dragons (Green, Red, White/Soap) On the 2026 Card Sections — What I Know and Where I Need Your Help Here's what I can speak to confidently, and where I want to be careful. What I know about American mahjong card structure generally The National Mah Jongg League card organizes hands into named sections, each with its own structural theme. Common section types that appear across card years include: 2468 — even-number hands only Any Like Numbers — same number across two or three suits Quints — five of the same tile (requires jokers) Consecutive Run — sequential numbers across one or more suits 13579 — odd-number hands only Winds and Dragons — hands built around wind and dragon tiles Singles and Pairs — hands made entirely of single tiles and pairs Addition Hands — numbers that add up to a target sum (e.g., tiles summing to a specific value) 367 / 369 / other number-combination sections — vary by year What I am NOT certain about The specific 2026 card — the exact section names, the exact hands within each section, how many hands per section, and any new sections introduced that year — I cannot confirm with certainty. The NMJL card changes annually and I don't want to build your logic system on assumptions that turn out to be wrong for 2026 specifically. THE COMPLETE REVISED LOGIC SYSTEM Mapped to the 2026 Card — MVP Version SECTION MAP — All 9 Sections Section Theme Jokers Allowed Exposure Difficulty 2026 Digits 2, 0, 2, 6 — White Dragon as zero Yes Mixed Medium 2468 Even numbers only Yes Mixed Easy–Medium Any Like Numbers Same number across suits Yes Mixed Easy–Medium Winds & Dragons Winds and dragons in groups Yes Mixed Medium–Hard Singles & Pairs All singles and pairs No All concealed Hardest 369 Groups of 3s, 6s, 9s Yes Mixed Medium Consecutive Run Sequential number runs Yes Mixed Medium 13579 Odd numbers only Yes Mixed Easy–Medium Quints Five of the same tile Yes (required) All open Hard TILE INPUT MODEL — Updated What the player selects Suited tiles: Bams 1–9, Dots 1–9, Cracks 1–9 (tap to add up to 4 of each) Winds: East, South, West, North (tap to add up to 4 of each) Dragons: Green, Red, White/Soap (tap to add up to 4 of each) Jokers: A simple +/− counter. Maximum 4 in a single hand (8 exist in the set but only 4 can appear in one player's hand realistically) Flowers: A simple +/− counter. Range 0–8. Flowers are available as a bonus signal but do not force any recommendation. Total tile count The app counts the 13 core tiles (suited + winds + dragons) separately from flowers and jokers. Flowers and jokers are supplementary inputs shown alongside but not counted in the 13. THE 5 PATTERN SIGNALS — Revised Signal What the app reads Suit concentration Which suit has the most tiles and how many Pairs Any tile appearing exactly twice Groups Any tile appearing 3 or 4 times (pungs and kongs) Wind/Dragon presence How many winds and dragons, and whether any are duplicated Flowers How many flowers held — boosts hands requiring FF or FFF Jokers Count only — each joker is a flexibility multiplier Sequences (consecutive runs) are now read as a sixth signal specifically for the Consecutive Run section. SECTION SCORING SYSTEM For each of the 9 sections, the app computes a section fit score based on how well the player's tiles align with that section's structural requirements. The top 3 sections by adjusted score become the recommendations. Base Scoring Factors (same for all sections) Factor Points Each tile that directly supports this section's structure +3 Each matching pair found +2 Each matching group of 3 (pung) found +4 Each matching group of 4 (kong) found +5 Flowers held when section uses flowers +3 per flower (max +6) Each joker (for joker-permitted sections) +2 Dominant suit concentration (6+ tiles in one suit) +5 bonus Consecutive run of 3+ in one suit +4 bonus (Consecutive Run section only) Concealed Penalty (applied after base score) Situation Penalty Section has some concealed hands −2 (mild signal to player) All hands in section are concealed −5 Singles & Pairs (concealed + no jokers) −8 Concealed Penalty Reductions Condition Reduction 8+ tiles already matching section structure −3 off penalty 1 joker held (joker-permitted sections only) −1 off penalty 2+ jokers held (joker-permitted sections only) −3 off penalty Pursuing Singles & Pairs with 2+ jokers No reduction — jokers don't help this section Flower Bonus (additive, never a penalty) If the player holds flowers and a section uses them, add the flower bonus to that section's score only. If the player holds flowers but a section doesn't use them, nothing happens — no penalty, no note to the player. SPECIAL RULES PER SECTION Singles & Pairs Joker bonus never applies — jokers are illegal in this section Full −8 penalty applies always Penalty can only be reduced by the strong-starting-tiles condition (8+ matching tiles) The app shows a strong warning note whenever this section appears in recommendations Only recommended if tile fit is exceptional — score must exceed 15 before penalty to surface in top 3 Quints Requires jokers by definition — if player holds 0 jokers, Quints is disqualified entirely Each joker is worth +4 instead of +2 for this section (jokers are structurally essential, not just helpful) Quints hands are all open so no concealed penalty applies 2026 White Dragon counts as a zero tile for this section — if player holds White Dragons, they receive the group bonus as if it were a suited tile Scoring checks for presence of 2s, 6s, and White Dragons specifically East and West winds boost score if present (one hand uses them) NEWS (all four winds) is checked as a separate sub-pattern Winds & Dragons Score is boosted by any duplicated wind or dragon (+3 per duplicated honor) A player holding 3+ of the same wind or 3+ of the same dragon gets a strong signal bonus (+5) Mixed wind/dragon hands and pure wind or pure dragon hands are both valid paths within this section Consecutive Run The app checks for the longest consecutive run in any single suit A run of 3 consecutive numbers = +4 A run of 4 consecutive numbers = +7 A run of 5 consecutive numbers = +10 If the player's longest run is only 2 consecutive numbers, Consecutive Run section gets no run bonus and is unlikely to surface in top 3 DISQUALIFICATION RULES A section is removed from consideration entirely if: Quints: player holds 0 jokers Any section: the player has zero tiles relevant to that section's structure AND zero jokers AND zero flowers that section uses Any section: adjusted score (after penalty) falls below 4 Singles & Pairs: base score before penalty is below 15 (too hard Apr 1 This is a great instinct and I think you've landed on exactly the right approach. Here's my recommendation: The Best Approach: Two-Track Recommendation Instead of penalizing a whole section, the app evaluates each hand path independently — open and concealed — and surfaces them separately when both are relevant. Here's how it works: Primary Recommendation — Always the Open Path The top 3 recommendations are always based on open hands first. No section gets penalized just because it contains some concealed hands. The player sees their strongest realistic paths — hands they can actively build by claiming discards. Secondary Note — "We Also Considered" When the player's tiles strongly suggest a specific concealed hand within a section, the app adds a short note beneath the relevant recommendation card. It doesn't replace the recommendation — it expands it. It looks like this: 🥇 2468 — Fit score: 32 [Normal recommendation content here] ───────────────────────── ALSO CONSIDERED There is a concealed hand in this section that fits your tiles well. It was ranked lower because concealed hands are harder to complete — you cannot claim any discards. Worth watching if your wall draws go well in the first few rounds. ───────────────────────── This gives the player full information without misleading them about difficulty. When Does the "Also Considered" Note Appear? Only when two conditions are both true: The player's tiles match the structural pattern of a known concealed hand within that section reasonably well — meaning 6 or more of their 13 tiles directly support that specific hand structure The concealed hand scores within 5 points of the open hands in the same section — meaning it's genuinely close and worth mentioning If the concealed hand is a weak match, it's ignored silently. The note only appears when it's actually relevant to that player's specific tiles. Singles & Pairs — Still Treated Differently Singles & Pairs remains its own special case because every hand is concealed and jokers are banned — it's not "a section with one concealed hand," it's an entirely different level of difficulty. It still gets the −8 penalty and the strong warning note when it surfaces. The "Also Considered" pattern doesn't apply to Singles & Pairs because there are no open alternatives within the section to compare against. Why This Is Better Than Penalizing the Section Approach Problem Penalize the whole section Steers player away from great open hands because of one concealed option they weren't even going to pursue Ignore concealed hands entirely Player misses a legitimate path that might be their best option Two-track system Player sees their best open path AND gets honest information about the harder concealed alternative — they decide The two-track system treats the player as an adult who can weigh the tradeoff themselves. The app's job is to give them complete, honest information — not to make the decision for them. How This Changes the Build In the hand library JSON, each hand pattern gets one new field: json { "hand_id": "2468-07", "section": "2468", "exposure_type": "concealed", "also_considered_eligible": true } The scoring engine runs two passes: Score all open hands → produce top 3 recommendations For each recommended section, check if any concealed hand in that section scores within 5 points of the open winner → if yes, attach the "Also Considered" note to that card The player always sees clean top 3 cards. The additional context appears only when it genuinely applies. Does this feel right to you? Concealed hands take a difficulty deduction, but if the tile fit is strong enough, a concealed hand can rank 1st, 2nd, or 3rd. The player just sees a clear label telling them it's concealed and what that means. The Right Penalty Size The penalty needs to be large enough to reflect real difficulty but small enough that a strong tile fit can overcome it. Here's the calibration: Situation Difficulty Deduction Open hand 0 Concealed hand, jokers allowed −4 Concealed hand, no jokers (Singles & Pairs) −8 Why −4 for standard concealed hands If a player's tile fit scores a 20 on an open hand and an 18 on a concealed hand before the penalty, the open hand wins by 2. After the −4 penalty the concealed hand sits at 14 — correctly ranked lower despite close tile fit. But if the same player scores a 26 on the concealed hand before penalty, it lands at 22 after deduction and wins the top spot. That's the right outcome — the tiles are so strong that the difficulty is worth it. Penalty Reductions Still Apply Condition Reduction 8+ tiles already matching the concealed hand −2 off penalty 2+ jokers in hand −2 off penalty Both conditions true −4 off penalty, concealed penalty effectively gone So a player with a near-complete concealed hand and two jokers competes on almost equal footing with open hands. A player with a moderate concealed fit and no jokers feels the full −4. That's accurate to real life. What the Player Sees The recommendation card looks the same regardless of rank. The Two Approaches Approach A: Recommend the Section The app recommends 2468 or Consecutive Run or Singles & Pairs — and within that section the player figures out which specific hand to target as their tiles develop. Pros: Much simpler to build for MVP Matches how experienced players actually think — they identify a section direction first, then narrow to a specific hand as draws come in Doesn't require the app to know every exact hand on the card More flexible — the player isn't locked into one specific hand early Cons: Less precise — a section might have 8 hands and the player still has to figure out which one Concealed/open distinction gets murky because one section can have both Approach B: Recommend the Specific Hand The app recommends a specific hand pattern — like "the FF 2222 4444 6666 8888 hand in 2468" — and scores each hand individually. KEY RULES THAT AFFECT THE APP Joker Rules — Important Clarifications Several joker rules from the card directly affect how we score hands: Jokers can NEVER replace: Any single tile Any tile in a pair Singles and Pairs section (already knew this) This is bigger than I had modeled. It means jokers only help with pungs, kongs, quints, and sextets — groups of 3 or more of the same tile. A hand that is built primarily from singles and pairs gets zero joker benefit even if it's not in the Singles & Pairs section. Practical impact on scoring: Any hand with a structure that is mostly singles and pairs — even in other sections — should not receive the joker bonus for those tile positions. Only the pung/kong/quint portions of a hand benefit from jokers. What This Changes in the Hand Library Some hands I modeled contain a mix of groups and pairs. The joker bonus should now only apply to the group portions. For example: 2468-02: EE 22 444 666 88 WW EE, 22, 88, WW are all pairs → jokers cannot help these 444 and 666 are pungs → jokers can help here Joker bonus applies only to the pung tiles, not the pairs This doesn't change the hand structure but it changes how many tiles in each hand actually benefit from jokers during scoring. Tile Definitions Confirmed From the rules page I can now confirm the full tile notation used on the card: Symbol Meaning F Flower X Exposed (point value marker) C Concealed (higher point value) D Dragon Pair–2 Any 2 like tiles Pung–3 Any 3 like tiles Kong–4 Any 4 like tiles Quint–5 Any 5 like tiles Sextet–6 Any 6 like tiles (1 color = 1 suit) Run Any consecutive numbers Matching Dragons Craks/Reds, Bams/Greens, Dots/Whites (Soaps) Matching Dragon rule confirmed: Red dragon matches Cracks, Green dragon matches Bams, White/Soap dragon matches Dots. This matters for hands that specify a matching dragon — the app needs to check suit against dragon color. Sextets The rules mention Sextet = 6 of the same tile. I see this referenced in the Quints section of the card. This means some hands can use 6 of the same tile across suits. I'll add this as a group type in the hand library where applicable. Charleston Rules — Relevant to App Context The Charleston allows passing 3 tiles per round. This is important context for the app: Players start with 13 tiles after the Charleston The app analyzes post-Charleston tiles — which is exactly what we designed for Blind passes are allowed, meaning some players may not know what they passed The app should note in its UI that tile entry is based on the player's hand after the Charleston is complete Dead Hand Rules A hand is dead if it has too few or too many tiles or contains an incorrect exposure. KEY THINGS I LEARNED FROM RESEARCH Colors on the Card = Number of Suits This is critical and I wasn't fully applying it. The colors on the NMJL card are NOT tied to specific suits. Instead, the colors indicate how many suits need to be in your hand. All one color means all one suit. Two colors means two different suits. Three colors means three different suits. Southern Sparrow This is how the engine must read every hand — count the colors, that tells you the suit requirement. Jokers — Confirmed and Expanded Jokers can substitute for a regular tile, but only in combinations of 3 or more tiles — pungs, kongs, quints — never for singles or pairs. Sequences like NEWS or 2026, though represented together on the card, are really 4 single tiles, so you cannot use a joker for these. I Love Mahj This is huge — the 2026 digit sequence and NEWS wind sequence get zero joker benefit. Any Like Numbers — Correctly Understood Now The Like Numbers category features hands made up entirely of tiles bearing the same number, regardless of suit. While the card may illustrate these hands using the number 1, you're free to use any number — 2s, 5s, or 9s — as long as all number tiles match. Mahj Mind The engine tests all 9 numbers and returns the best fit automatically. Confirmed. Consecutive Run — The Suit Split Clarified A hand shown in two colors means the tiles must come from two different suits. For example: 444 555 6666 777 in two colors means the sixes and sevens must be one suit and the eights and nines must be another suit. Google Sites So the color coding on the card directly tells you which tile groups must share a suit. This is exactly what you were describing — the engine must track color groupings per hand, not just overall suit count. Opposing Dragons — Confirmed Consecutive Run hands that require opposite dragons need all three suits to be represented, so if the number tiles in one suit are the same, the corresponding dragons of the other two suits must be used. Mahj Life So opposing dragon means the dragon that matches whichever suit is NOT being used for the number tiles. Concealed Hands — One Important Exception If you are playing a concealed hand, you CAN call for the 14th tile if it results in Mah Jongg. Ateacherfirst So concealed doesn't mean wall-only for the very last tile — you can claim a discard to win. This doesn't change the scoring engine but it's a useful note to show players in the recommendation output. Singles and Pairs — Each Hand Mirrors Another Section Each Singles and Pairs hand corresponds to one of the main categories, allowing you to switch strategies mid-game if your Singles and Pairs hand becomes impossible to complete. Mahj Mind This is a great learning note to surface in the app — if a player is matching a Singles and Pairs hand, tell them which open-hand section it mirrors as a fallback. 14-Tile Confirmation You should have 13 tiles most of the time and 14 tiles when it's your turn or when you call Mah Jongg. I Love Mahj Confirms East starts with 14 — our design is correct. Apr 2 Exactly right — and that's an important distinction for the engine. Singles are hard to acquire from the wall or discards — you can never claim a discard to complete a single, and drawing one specific tile you need is low probability. But singles you already hold after the Charleston are an asset — they directly reduce the difficulty of any hand that needs them. How This Changes the Scoring Engine The difficulty rating for singles-dependent hands is not fixed. It's dynamic based on what the player is already holding. Situation Engine behavior Hand needs singles, player holds none of them High difficulty penalty applied Hand needs singles, player holds some of them Penalty reduced proportionally Hand needs singles, player holds ALL of them No singles-related penalty at all So CR Line 8 — which needs two copies of single-N in two different suits — goes from maximum difficulty to much more achievable if the player is already holding both of those singles after the Charleston. Same logic applies to: The 2026 digit sequence (2, 0, 2, 6 singles) NEWS tiles in the Winds & Dragons and 2026 sections Any singles in Singles & Pairs hands The Clean Rule for the Engine Difficulty ratings reflect acquisition difficulty, not holding difficulty. If you already have it, it's not hard anymore. The engine checks which required singles the player holds and reduces the difficulty signal accordingly — tile by tile. Tile Counts in a Standard American Mahjong Set Tile Type Count in Full Set Each numbered tile (e.g. 3-Bam) 4 Each dragon (Green, Red, White) 4 each = 12 total Each wind (N, E, W, S) 4 each = 16 total Flowers 8 total Jokers 8 total Total tiles 152 Why This Matters for the Engine If the player needs a tile they don't have, the engine needs to know how many of that tile exist in the world and how many are realistically still available. Starting availability for any tile the player doesn't hold: Tile needed Total in set Player holds 0 Player holds 1 Player holds 2 Player holds 3 Any numbered tile 4 4 available 3 available 2 available 1 available Any dragon 4 4 available 3 available 2 available 1 available Any wind 4 4 available 3 available 2 available 1 available Flower 8 8 available 7 available 6 available 5 available Joker 8 8 available 7 available 6 available 5 available The Probability Problem In a 4-player game, after the Charleston: Each player holds 13 tiles That's 52 tiles in players' hands Plus tiles passed and received during Charleston Remaining wall has approximately 152 - 52 - 14 (East's extra + dead tiles) = roughly 72-80 tiles in the wall at game start So if you need a tile and hold 0 of it: 4 copies exist somewhere across other players' hands and the wall You have no visibility into other players' hands You can only access wall tiles on your turn OR claim discards (for pungs/kongs/quints only) How Deep to Go for MVP There are two options: Option A — Simple availability signal (recommended for MVP) The engine uses a straightforward availability score based on how many copies of the needed tile the player already holds: Player holds Copies remaining Availability signal 0 of 4 4 remaining Easy to find 1 of 4 3 remaining Good 2 of 4 2 remaining Moderate 3 of 4 1 remaining Hard 0 of 4 but need 3+ 4 remaining but need many Very hard This is fast, simple, and honest without requiring probability math. Option B — True probability calculation Calculate actual draw probability based on: Tiles needed vs. tiles remaining in wall Number of draws player will likely get Accounting for other players competing for same tiles This is more accurate but significantly more complex to build and explain to users. My Recommendation Option A for MVP — with one important addition. When the engine calculates missing tiles for a hand, it also checks: How many copies are needed — needing 4 of a tile (kong) when you hold 0 is very different from needing 2 (pair) How many you already hold — determines remaining copies in the wild Competition pressure — flowers and jokers have 8 copies so they're easier to find; specific numbered tiles have only 4 so competition is real The engine surfaces this as a simple plain-English note: "You need 3 more Green Dragons — only 4 exist in the set. This is a competitive tile." "You need 2 more flowers — 8 exist in the set, giving you good odds." "You need a kong of 6-Bams and hold none — 4 exist but you need all 4. Very difficult." Option B can be added in v2 once the core recommendation engine is proven. Apr 2 Exactly right — and that's a really sharp observation. Flowers are not low-competition just because there are 8 of them. They are high-demand across the entire card, which means every player at the table may be holding or chasing them simultaneously. The Competition Factor Every tile needs two scores, not one: 1. Availability — how many exist in the set minus what the player holds 2. Competition pressure — how many OTHER hands on the card also need this tile, which predicts how many other players at the table are likely chasing it Competition Pressure by Tile Type Tile Copies in set Demand across card Competition level Flowers 8 Every section has flower hands Very High Jokers 8 Nearly every hand benefits Very High 6s (any suit) 4 per suit 2026, 2468, 369, ALN, CR all use 6s Very High 2s (any suit) 4 per suit 2026, 2468, ALN, CR High Dragons 4 per dragon Most sections use dragons High Winds 4 per wind Winds & Dragons, 2026 NEWS hand Medium 1s 4 per suit 13579, CR only Low-Medium 9s 4 per suit 13579, CR only Low-Medium How the Engine Uses This For each missing tile in a recommended hand, the engine computes a tile pressure score combining both factors: tile_pressure = (copies_needed - copies_held) × competition_weight competition_weight: flowers → 2.0 (highest competition) jokers → 2.0 (highest competition) 6s → 1.8 (very high cross-section demand) 2s → 1.6 (high cross-section demand) dragons → 1.4 (moderate-high) winds → 1.2 (moderate) 3s,5s,8s → 1.1 (moderate cross-section demand) 1s,9s → 1.0 (lowest competition) Higher tile pressure = harder to complete = lower recommendation score. These are in lower demand — reasonable odds of drawing it." Medium pressure tile: "You need two more Green Dragons. Dragons are moderately competitive — watch what others are discarding." High pressure tile: "You need three more flowers. Flowers are in high demand across the entire card — every player at the table may be holding them. Factor this into your Charleston strategy." Very high pressure — specific number: "You need a kong of 6-Cracks and hold none. Sixes are the most contested tile on the 2026 card — appearing in nearly 40% of all hands. Be cautious about committing to this path without more 6s in hand." Apr 2 You're right — and I need to correct something I said in the competition weight table. I listed jokers at 2.0 competition weight as if they're just generally useful. But jokers are more specific than that — and more powerful. Corrected Joker Rule Jokers can substitute for any tile in ANY pung, kong, quint, or sextet — regardless of suit, number, wind, or dragon. This means: A joker can be a 6-Bam in a pung of 6-Bams A joker can be a Green Dragon in a kong of Green Dragons A joker can be a North Wind in a pung of North Winds A joker can be a flower in a kong of flowers — wait, actually flowers don't form pungs/kongs in hands, they're just counted individually So the only things jokers cannot do: Replace a single tile Replace either tile in a pair Be used in the 2026 digit sequence (singles) Be used in NEWS (singles) Be used in any pair position anywhere on the card Be used in Singles & Pairs section at all What This Means for the Engine Every time the engine calculates missing tiles for a hand, it should first check: "Can a joker fill this gap?" Position type Joker can help? Pung — need 1 more Yes Pung — need 2 more Yes — up to 2 jokers Kong — need 1-3 more Yes Quint — need 1-4 more Yes — always needs at least 1 Single — need 1 No Pair — need 1 more No Pair — need both No So the engine's missing tile calculation becomes: for each missing tile position in a hand: if position_type is pung/kong/quint: check if player has available jokers each joker covers one missing tile in this position reduce missing_tile_count accordingly if position_type is single or pair: jokers cannot help missing_tile_count stays as-is apply acquisition difficulty based on tile competition weight The Practical Impact on Recommendations A hand that needs 2 more tiles for a kong but the player has 2 jokers — that hand is effectively complete for that group. The engine should treat it as zero missing tiles for that position. A hand that needs 1 more tile for a pair and the player has 3 jokers — those jokers are completely useless for that gap. The engine treats it as 1 genuinely missing tile with full acquisition difficulty applied. This is what makes joker count so critical to the recommendation quality — not just "more jokers = better" but specifically which positions in each hand can absorb jokers. Ready for Any Like Numbers Line 4 now. I think so. People like to keep their jokers flexible. There might be multiple sections of the hand that can use a joker to people keep that in mind and see how the hands go. keep that in mind Apr 2 Perfect — and that's a really important strategic nuance. Joker Flexibility Principle Players don't commit jokers to a specific position early. They hold jokers as flexible assets and decide where to deploy them as the hand develops. What this means for the engine: Don't assign jokers to specific positions during analysis. Instead, count the total number of joker-eligible positions in a hand and report that as the hand's joker flexibility score — how many different places a joker could go. How the Engine Uses This For each recommended hand the engine calculates: Total joker-eligible positions — every pung, kong, or quint slot that could absorb a joker Then surfaces this to the player as a flexibility signal: High flexibility (3+ eligible positions): "This hand has multiple places your joker(s) can go — you don't need to commit early. Let the hand develop and place them where they're needed most." Medium flexibility (2 eligible positions): "Your joker has two useful spots in this hand. Hold it until you see which group develops more slowly." Low flexibility (1 eligible position): "This hand has only one place for a joker. If that group completes naturally, your joker becomes less useful here." Zero flexibility (all pairs and singles): "Jokers cannot help with this hand at all. Every position requires exact tiles." This also affects how the engine compares two hands with similar tile fit scores — the one with higher joker flexibility ranks higher, because it gives the player more strategic options as the game develops. Ready for Any Like Numbers Line 4. Remember that you can only call for a single or a pair for mahjong. Apr 2 Critical rule — and it refines everything. The Complete Calling Rule Position type Can you call a discard for exposure? Can you call a discard for Mahjong? Pung Yes Yes Kong Yes Yes Quint Yes Yes Single No Yes Pair No Yes So singles and pairs are never completely unreachable from discards — they just can't be claimed mid-game for exposure. The one exception is the final tile to win. What This Changes in the Engine Previously I was treating singles and pairs as wall-draw-only tiles for their entire acquisition. That was wrong. The correct framing is: Singles and pairs are wall-draw-only UNTIL the player is one tile from Mahjong. At that point, ANY tile — single, pair, pung, kong, quint — can be claimed from a discard to win. How the Engine Reflects This The difficulty signal for singles and pairs now has two components: 1. Mid-game acquisition difficulty — high, because you can't claim discards to build them during play. Every single and pair must come from the wall until you're ready to win. 2. Winning tile accessibility — normal, because the final tile can be any type including a single or pair claimed from any discard. So the engine adjusts its language: Old (wrong): "Singles are hard — you can never claim them from discards." New (correct): "Singles must be drawn from the wall during play — you can't claim discards to build them. However, if a single is your final winning tile, you can claim it from any discard to go Mahjong." This particularly affects: CR Line 8 (concealed — singles + pairs throughout) Singles & Pairs section (all singles and pairs) 2026 digit sequences NEWS tiles Any hand with singles mid-structure The difficulty of these hands is real — but not absolute. The engine notes that the final tile is always claimable, which slightly reduces the difficulty signal for hands where the player is close to completion. The Final Complete Calling Rules Position type Exposed hand — mid game Exposed hand — Mahjong Concealed hand — mid game Concealed hand — Mahjong Pung Yes Yes No Yes Kong Yes Yes No Yes Quint Yes Yes No Yes Single No Yes No Yes Pair No Yes No Yes One rule covers everything for concealed hands: you cannot claim ANY discard during play. The only exception is the final tile to win — and that applies to any tile type. What This Means for the Engine Concealed hands now have a unified difficulty signal — not broken down by position type, because the restriction applies equally to everything: For concealed hands: Every tile in this hand except the final winning tile must come from the wall. This applies to pungs, kongs, singles, and pairs equally. Your jokers help with pungs and kongs but not singles or pairs — and you cannot claim any discard until you are ready to win. For exposed hands: Pungs, kongs, and quints can be claimed from discards during play. Singles and pairs must come from the wall — except your final winning tile, which can be claimed from any discard. This is now the complete and accurate calling framework. Everything else we've built is consistent with this. Ready for Any Like Numbers Line 4. do you have any other questions to nail down the difficulty rating? It's taking the number of tiles that a person has, and then choosing the best hands based on the likelihood that they will be able to pull for what they need. So they could have 9 of 14 tiles that they need to complete a hand but maybe a hand where they only have 7 tiles towards would be better bc it's easier to get what you need. does that make sense? how do we accoutn for that Apr 2 Yes — that makes complete sense and it's the most important insight in the whole engine. Tile fit alone is not enough. Achievability is what actually matters. The Core Problem A player might have 9 of 14 tiles for Hand A and 7 of 14 for Hand B. But Hand B could be the better recommendation because the 7 remaining tiles needed are: Available in higher quantities Lower competition from other players Joker-fillable positions Claimable from discards (exposed hand) While Hand A's 5 remaining tiles might be: Singles that can't be claimed High competition tiles like flowers or 6s Pair positions jokers can't fill Concealed hand so nothing can be claimed The Two-Part Score Every hand gets scored on two dimensions that combine into one final ranking score: Part 1 — Tile Fit Score How many of the 14 required tiles does the player already hold? tile_fit = tiles_held / tiles_required Part 2 — Acquisition Score For each missing tile, how hard is it to get? For every missing tile position the engine evaluates: for each missing tile: 1. Can a joker fill it? → Yes (pung/kong/quint): joker_coverage = jokers_available → No (single/pair): joker_coverage = 0 2. Can it be claimed from a discard? → Exposed hand + pung/kong/quint: claimable = true → Exposed hand + single/pair: claimable = false (except final tile) → Concealed hand: claimable = false (except final tile) 3. How many copies exist that player doesn't hold? → copies_available = total_in_set - player_holds 4. How competitive is this tile? → apply competition_weight from tile pressure table 5. Combine into a per-tile difficulty score: difficulty = competition_weight / (copies_available + joker_coverage + claimable_bonus) Then sum all per-tile difficulty scores into a total acquisition difficulty for the hand. The Final Ranking Formula final_score = tile_fit_score - acquisition_difficulty higher final_score = better recommendation This means: A hand with 9/14 tiles but hard remaining tiles could score lower than a hand with 7/14 tiles but easy remaining tiles Jokers directly improve acquisition score for eligible positions Exposed hands score better than concealed hands for the same tile fit High competition tiles like flowers and 6s penalize hands that still need them Singles and pairs that are missing penalize harder than missing kong tiles Simple Example Hand A: 9/14 tiles held Missing: 2 singles (can't claim, no joker help, competition weight 1.5) Missing: 1 pair (can't claim, no joker help, competition weight 1.8) Missing: 2 kong tiles (can claim, jokers can help, competition weight 1.3) Concealed hand Hand B: 7/14 tiles held Missing: 4 kong tiles across 2 groups (can claim from discards, jokers cover 2 of them) Missing: 2 flower positions (jokers can help, 8 in set) Missing: 1 pung tile (claimable, moderate competition) Exposed hand Hand B wins despite worse tile fit — because every missing tile is either joker-fillable, claimable, or both. Flowers need a balanced treatment — not penalized too hard, not treated as easy grabs either. The Flower Balance Rule Flowers have two competing factors that partially cancel each other out: Factor 1 — Higher availability: 8 in the set vs. 4 for every other tile. If a player holds 2 flowers, 6 still exist. That's meaningfully better odds than a numbered tile where only 2 remain. Factor 2 — Higher competition: Every section has flower hands. Multiple players at the table are likely holding or chasing flowers simultaneously. High availability doesn't help if other players are absorbing all of them. How the Engine Handles This Flowers get a split competition weight that reflects both factors: flower_availability_bonus = 0.7 (more copies in set than any other tile) flower_competition_penalty = 1.5 (highest cross-section demand on the card) net_flower_weight = 1.5 - 0.7 = 0.8 So flowers end up with a moderate net difficulty — harder than low-demand tiles like 1s and 9s, but easier than high-demand low-availability tiles like 6s where only 4 exist and competition is fierce. Let me be completely transparent. How to Read the Weights The weight is a competition score — higher means harder to get. It combines two things: Demand — how many hands on the card explicitly need this tile Availability — how many copies exist in the set A tile that appears in 35% of hands but has 12 copies in the set scores lower than a tile that appears in 16% of hands but only has 4 copies. That's why winds score as high as 6s despite appearing in fewer hands — only 4 of each wind exist vs. 12 of each number. Your Three Problems Are Exactly Right Problem 1 — Flexible sections not counted fairly For hands like Consecutive Run ("any 3 consecutive numbers") and Any Like Numbers ("any number 1-9"), I gave zero credit to any specific number because no specific number is locked in. This is correct for those hands — but it means numbers like 6 are undercounted because I only counted hands where 6 is explicitly required. In reality, when a player pursues a consecutive run of 4-5-6-7, the 6 becomes just as demanded as in an explicit hand. For MVP the fairest approach is to give each number an equal fractional credit for flexible hands. So a "any 3 consecutive numbers" hand gives each of the 9 possible starting points equal weight, meaning each number gets 1/7th credit (numbers 1-9 can each appear in runs, with middle numbers appearing more often). Actually this gets complex fast. For MVP I recommend we use the explicit counts as the baseline and add a flexibility bonus of +0.1 to all numbers 3-7 since they appear in more possible consecutive runs than 1,2,8,9. Problem 2 — White Dragon only counted for 2026 hands Yes — you caught this exactly. I only counted white dragon in hands where it appears explicitly as a zero (6 hands — the 2026 section and SP Line 6). But white dragon also appears as: A matching dragon for Dots in many hands An opposing dragon in several hands Part of "any dragon" groups throughout the card So white dragon is severely undercounted at 6 hands. It should be folded into the dragon counting properly. Problem 3 — Any Dragon double counting Yes — there is a double counting problem. I tracked: white_dragon separately for explicit zero uses any_dragon as a catch-all for all dragon groups This means some hands got counted twice and others got missed entirely. The three dragons need to be tracked cleanly as: Green Dragon — appears whenever Bams are the suit + any dragon groups Red Dragon — appears whenever Cracks are the suit + any dragon groups White Dragon — appears whenever Dots are the suit + any dragon groups + zero in 2026 When a hand says "any dragon" each specific dragon gets 1/3 credit since all three are equally valid The Correct Approach Let me recount properly: Corrected tile competition weight calculation Corrected tile competition weight calculation Now the numbers are honest and clean. Apr 4 Yes — and this is exactly the kind of precision that makes the app genuinely useful vs. just a lookup tool. How the Engine Handles This When the engine evaluates a hand that requires an opposing dragon, it doesn't just check "does the player have a dragon." It checks the specific logic: Step 1 — Identify the suit the player is using for that hand If the player has 6 Bams and the engine is scoring a hand built around Bams, it knows the primary suit is Bams. Step 2 — Determine which dragons are opposing If primary suit = Bams (Green matches) → Opposing dragons = Red and White If primary suit = Cracks (Red matches) → Opposing dragons = Green and White If primary suit = Dots (White matches) → Opposing dragons = Green and Red Step 3 — Check what dragon the player actually holds If the player holds a Green Dragon but the hand needs an opposing dragon to Bams — Green is the MATCHING dragon, not the opposing dragon. It is completely useless for this hand. The engine marks Green Dragon as: Zero contribution to this hand's dragon requirement Not counted as a tile toward completion Potentially flagged as a tile to discard What the Player Sees 🥇 369 Line 3b — Fit score: 18 Achievability: Medium What's working: Strong Bam concentration — pung of 3-Bam, pung of 6-Bam, pair of 9-Bam Dragon note: This hand needs an opposing dragon (Red or White). You are holding a Green Dragon — that is the MATCHING dragon for Bams and cannot be used here. You still need a Red or White Dragon. What you still need: 1 Red or White Dragon for the kong This Also Works in Reverse If a hand needs a matching dragon and the player holds the wrong color — same logic applies in reverse. Example: Player has Red Dragon, hand needs matching dragon for Dots — Red is opposing to Dots, not matching. Engine marks it as zero contribution and flags it. The Dragon Lookup Table in the Engine javascript const matchingDragon = { "BAM": "GREEN", "CRK": "RED", "DOT": "WHITE" } const opposingDragons = { "BAM": ["RED", "WHITE"], "CRK": ["GREEN", "WHITE"], "DOT": ["GREEN", "RED"] } function dragonHelpsHand(playerDragon, handSuit, handRequires) { if (handRequires === "matching") { return playerDragon === matchingDragon[handSuit] } if (handRequires === "opposing") { return opposingDragons[handSuit].includes(playerDragon) } if (handRequires === "any") { return true // any dragon works } return false } This function runs for every dragon tile in the player's hand against every hand being scored. A dragon either helps or it doesn't — no credit for the wrong color. One Additional Nuance For hands like WD Line 3 where three dragon groups span three suits — the engine checks whether the player's specific dragons can fill the three required dragon pungs across three different suits. Holding one Green and needing three dragon pungs across three suits means the Green covers Bams, but Red and White still need to be found for the other two suits. Rule 2 — Trigger tile logic For each pivot candidate the engine identifies the specific tile or tiles that would move it into the top 3. That becomes the "when to switch" trigger shown to the player. javascript function getPivotTrigger(pivotHand, playerTiles) { // Find which missing tile would most improve this hand's score // That tile becomes the "watch for" trigger const missingTiles = getMissingTiles(pivotHand, playerTiles) const highImpactTile = missingTiles .sort((a,b) => b.scoreImpact - a.scoreImpact)[0] return { tile: highImpactTile, message: `If you draw ${highImpactTile.description}, this hand moves into your top 3` } } The Educational Philosophy Every part of the output teaches something: Output element What it teaches Why this fits Pattern recognition — what makes a hand viable What's working Which tiles are assets vs. dead weight Dragon notes Matching vs. opposing — a common source of confusion Achievability Open vs. concealed — real-world difficulty Pivot hands Flexibility — when and why to switch Dead tiles Discard strategy — what to pass Trigger tiles What to watch for — active learning during play Maximum Pivot Hands Shown I'd recommend 2-3 pivot hands maximum. More than that becomes overwhelming and defeats the purpose of the top 3 recommendation. The player should always have a clear primary direction plus a small set of realistic alternatives. Apr 4 Of course. Let me strip it all the way down. Competition Score — Simple Version Imagine you're at a yard sale and you want to buy a specific lamp. If only 2 people want that lamp — you'll probably get it. If 20 people want that lamp — good luck. That's competition score. For every tile you still need, the app asks: "How many other players at this table probably want this same tile?" If lots of hands on the card use 6s — and they do, 6s appear in more hands than any other number — then all four players might be collecting 6s. That makes 6s hard to get even though there are 12 in the set. If hardly any hands use 4s, your opponents are probably throwing them away. Easy to get. That's it. Higher competition = harder to get = lower recommendation score. Difficulty Penalty — Simple Version Now imagine two paths to win: Path A: You need 3 more tiles. You can grab them off the table when other players throw them away. Easy. Path B: You need 3 more tiles. You can ONLY get them by drawing from the wall on your turn. You can never grab anyone else's discards. Hard. Same number of tiles needed. Completely different difficulty. That's the difficulty penalty. The app looks at each hand and asks: "Can this player grab tiles off the table, or are they stuck waiting for lucky draws?" The Three Difficulty Questions For every tile still needed, the app asks three things in order: Question 1 — Do you already have a joker that covers this gap? If yes — problem solved. No penalty. Move on. Question 2 — Can you grab this tile when someone throws it away? If the hand is open AND the tile goes into a pung, kong, or quint — yes you can. If the tile goes into a single or pair — no you can't, even in an open hand. If the hand is concealed — no you can't, for anything. Question 3 — How many other people probably want this tile? High competition tile = high penalty. Low competition tile = low penalty. Put It Together — One Example You have 8 Bam tiles after the Charleston. You're looking at two hands: Hand A — Open hand, needs a kong of 6-Bams You have 2 six-Bams already Need 2 more You can claim them from discards Joker covers one gap 6s are competitive but you only need 1 more naturally Low penalty — realistic hand Hand B — Concealed hand, needs singles of 3, 5, 7 in Bams You have none of these Can't claim any from discards — concealed hand Jokers can't help — they're singles 3s, 5s, 7s are all moderately competitive Must draw all three naturally from the wall on your turn High penalty — hard hand Even if your tiles match Hand B slightly better on paper — Hand A wins the recommendation because you can actually complete it. The Simple Summary Competition score = how many other players want the same tiles you need Difficulty penalty = how hard it is to GET the tiles you're missing The app combines both into one honest answer: "This hand fits your tiles AND you can realistically complete it." That's what makes it different from just showing you every possible match. ok. now walk me through the actual scores for each tile. Mahjong advisor Code · HTML Everything has to be counted within their suit. Let me think through exactly what this means for every tile type. The Complete Suit Rule Numbers — already correctly suit-specific (6-Bam ≠ 6-Crack) Flowers — no suit, neutral ✓ (already correct) Jokers — no suit, substitute for anything ✓ (already correct) Dragons — each dragon IS its suit: Green Dragon = Bam suit Red Dragon = Crack suit White Dragon = Dot suit (or zero — neutral only in 2026 hands) So 1 Red Dragon + 1 White Dragon are NOT interchangeable. Bug 1 — Joker being committed to specific groups The joker budget is being consumed group by group as the engine loops through. So if the first pung needs 1 more tile, the joker gets assigned there — and then later groups show as fully missing even if the joker could have helped them more. The joker should NOT be assigned to any specific group during scoring. It should simply reduce the overall difficulty of the hand as a flexible asset. Bug 2 — False credit for tiles I don't have You have 2-Bam x2. That's a pair. The engine is somehow crediting a pung of 2-Bam AND using the joker on top. The engine should show 0 tiles toward that group, period. The joker was already used on the Pung-2 group, so there's nothing left to cover the Pung-6. But if jokers aren't committed to specific groups anymore, then the Pung-6 would show as 0 tiles, 3 needed — which is honest. The Design Principle — Joker is a flexibility multiplier, not a group filler During analysis: Score each group based ONLY on tiles the player actually holds Count total tiles matched across all groups (no joker) Count total tiles needed to complete the hand (no joker) Joker count reduces the final difficulty score — fewer jokers = higher penalty, more jokers = lower penalty In the output, show joker separately: "You have X joker(s) — X fewer tiles you need to draw" This way the joker never gets falsely committed to one group at the expense of another, and the player sees an honest picture of what they actually hold. Remove joker from group-by-group scoring entirely - treat as flexibility multiplier on final score only Remove joker from group-by-group scoring entirely - treat as flexibility multiplier on final score only Claude's response was interrupted Please let me respond before you start fixing stuff since my limit is almost up. When a hand needs both a pair AND a pung of the same tile in the same suit, the pair gets filled first from the player's tiles, because pairs are harder to complete and can't use jokers. So with 2x 2-Bam: Pair of 2-Bam → filled ✓ (uses both tiles) Pung of 2-Bam → 0 tiles remaining toward it (the 2 tiles were already consumed by the pair) The engine currently has no concept of tile consumption priority across groups. It scores each group independently against the full tile count, which means the same 2 tiles get counted toward both the pair AND the pung — double counting. The Fix Required The engine needs to process groups in priority order and consume tiles as it goes: Priority order: Singles first — exact tiles, no joker, hardest to replace Pairs — exact tiles, no joker, very hard Pungs — jokers can help, claimable Kongs — jokers can help, claimable Quints — jokers required When the same tile appears in multiple groups (pair + pung of 2-Bam), the pair consumes its tiles first, leaving the pung to score against what remains. For example, if a hand needs 4 green dragons, and you only have 2 and no jokers and someone throws out a green dragon, you can't pick it up. If you have 2 green and 1 joker, then you can call it and show that exposure of 4 green dragons. That's why jokers are so valuable. So if someone throws out what you need early in the game and you don't have the rest of the section you need, you're somewhat screwed. Apr 5 This is a critical rule that changes the "can call" logic significantly. Let me make sure I understand it completely: The Claiming Rule You can only claim a discard if claiming it completes the entire exposure — meaning after you pick it up, you can immediately lay down that group face-up. So to claim a tile for a group, you need: Pung (3): hold 2 of that tile (or 1 + 1 joker) → can claim the 3rd Kong (4): hold 3 of that tile (or 2 + 1 joker, or 1 + 2 jokers) → can claim the 4th Quint (5): hold 4 (or enough jokers to fill) → can claim the 5th So "can call" is NOT just about whether the group type is claimable — it's about whether the player already has enough tiles to complete that exposure right now if the discard comes. What This Changes in the Engine Currently the engine tags any pung/kong/quint position as can call regardless of how many tiles the player holds toward it. That's wrong. The correct logic: canCall = groupIsClaimable (not pair/single) AND (holds + jokers) >= (count - 1) Where count - 1 means "one short of complete" — because the discard provides the final tile. So for your 4 Green Dragon example: Hold 2 Green, 0 jokers → need 2 more → cannot call (2 away, not 1 away) Hold 2 Green, 1 joker → need 1 more → can call (joker fills one gap, discard fills the last) Hold 3 Green, 0 jokers → need 1 more → can call And This Affects the Joker Logic Too This is exactly why jokers are so valuable — they don't just reduce difficulty abstractly, they literally unlock claiming on groups you'd otherwise be stuck building from the wall. A player with 2 of a tile and 1 joker can call the 3rd for a pung or use the joker to get to 3 and call the 4th for a kong. Without the joker, they're stuck waiting to draw naturally. Two Follow-Up Questions 1. Joker allocation for calling — if a player has 1 joker and two incomplete groups (say, 2 Green Dragons needing 1 more, AND 2 West Winds needing 2 more), the joker can only unlock ONE of those calls. Should the engine pick the best use, or flag both as potentially callable with a note that the joker can only go to one? 2. Mid-game vs. post-Charleston — since this app analyzes right after the Charleston, the player hasn't called anything yet. Should "can call" mean "you're positioned to call this IF you draw one more first" (future state) or strictly "you could call this right now if someone threw it" (current state)? This distinction matters because post-Charleston a player might have 1 of 4 needed for a kong — they can't call yet, but they're building toward it. The current engine treats that as "can call" which is wrong per your rule. I think for question 1 you should flag both as callable. There is no way for anyone to know what will be discarded first and therefore where you will use that joker. It can only unlock one but then you can pull another joker after you start playing. For question two- you can call it throughout the game. You can't call it right away. I am positioning this as post chareleston but someone should be able to use this mid game too if they want. Apr 5 Perfect. That clarifies everything. So the rule is: Claiming — Final Logic Can call means: at any point during play, if someone discards this tile, you'll be able to claim it — because you either already have enough, or will have enough as you build. For the engine's purposes at post-Charleston analysis: canCall = group is pung/kong/quint (not pair or single) AND holds >= count - 1 - jokers Meaning: you need to be at most ONE tile away after jokers are applied. If you need 2 more real tiles with no jokers, you cannot call — you'd have to draw at least one naturally first. Example: Kong of 4 Green Dragons, you hold 2 Green + 1 joker → 2 + 1 = 3, one away → can call Kong of 4 Green Dragons, you hold 2 Green + 0 jokers → 2, two away → cannot call yet Pung of 3 West Winds, you hold 1 West + 1 joker → 1 + 1 = 2, one away → can call Joker flagging: If two groups both become callable only because of the same joker, flag both as can call with a note that the joker can only be used for one exposure — but another joker may come during play. Mid-game use: The app works post-Charleston as the primary use case, but since calling is available throughout the game, the can/cannot call logic reflects the full game arc, not just the current moment. Apr 5 Yes — this is a really important distinction and makes the advice much more actionable. Let me map out the three states: Three Claiming States Ready to call now You have enough tiles (plus jokers) that if someone discards this tile today, you pick it up and expose immediately. Pung needs 3: you hold 2 → ready Pung needs 3: you hold 1 + 1 joker → ready Kong needs 4: you hold 3 → ready Kong needs 4: you hold 2 + 1 joker → ready Can call but not yet — need more first The group is claimable in theory but you need to draw more tiles or jokers before a discard helps you. Kong needs 4: you hold 2, no jokers → need to draw 1 more before you can call Pung needs 3: you hold 1, no jokers → need to draw 1 more before you can call Must draw — cannot call at all Pairs and singles. No calling mid-game regardless of how many you hold. Scoring Impact Hands where more groups are ready to call now should score higher than hands where groups are claimable in theory but need drawing first. The distinction is: Ready to call = high value, someone else's discard solves your problem today Not yet callable = still depends on the wall first Must draw = wall only, no help from discards ever So the scoring bonus for "claimable" should only fully apply when the group is ready to call now. Groups that need more tiles before they become callable get a partial bonus — better than must-draw, not as good as ready-to-call. Output Language Ready to call now: Call from discards — ready in green Claimable but need more first: Build toward this first, then call in amber — with a note like "need 1 more 6-Crack or a joker before you can call" Must draw: Must draw — no calling in neutral One Question Before I Build For the ranking rationale sentence on each card — do you want it to explicitly call out the claiming state? Something like: "Ranked #1 — most tiles matched and two groups are ready to call right now." "Ranked #2 — solid tile match but your gaps need building before you can call into them." Singles Difficulty Compounding You're right — Singles & Pairs Line 3 has FIVE singles/pairs that must be drawn naturally, no jokers, no calling. But when you have 3, 4, 5 must-draw positions stacking up, the difficulty compounds exponentially in reality — you need to draw ALL of them, so each one reduces your probability of completing the hand multiplicatively. The fix: apply an exponential compounding penalty based on how many pair/single tiles are still needed. Something like: mustDrawCount = total tiles still needed in pair/single positions compoundMultiplier = 1 + (mustDrawCount * 0.15) So 1 must-draw tile = 1.15x, 3 tiles = 1.45x, 5 tiles = 1.75x on top of the base penalty. This means a hand with 5 singles to pull gets significantly more penalized than a hand with 1 pair to pull. Two quick questions: 1. Real tiles vary. do upper left Apr 5 Building the full tile set now. Claude's response was interrupted is there a way to make this look more like a drawing and not clip art? Apr 5 Yes — the difference is in how the shapes are drawn. Clip art uses perfect geometric shapes (rectangles, circles, clean fills). A drawing uses: Slightly irregular paths instead of perfect rectangles for bamboo Varying stroke weight — thicker in the middle, tapering at the ends, like a brush Ink line style — outlines with slightly imperfect edges rather than solid fills Subtle variation between stalks so they don't look copy-pasted Brushstroke quality on the dragons — thin flowing lines, not uniform strokes The best way to achieve this is to draw everything as elements with hand-tuned bezier curves, using stroke-based illustration (colored outlines, lighter fills) rather than solid filled shapes. Think of it like someone drew these with a fine ink pen and watercolor wash — the line is the art, the fill is just a light tint. Before I rebuild, let me confirm the approach: Bamboo — ink outline of a stalk, slightly organic curves, light green wash fill, darker ink nodes, tiny brush-stroke leaves Dots — hand-drawn concentric rings, slightly imperfect, like stamped circles Crack (萬) — brushstroke character, slightly varied weight Dragon — fine ink line, minimal, elegant. Think a few confident brush strokes that suggest a dragon without overdrawing it Flowers — delicate petal outlines, ink style Does this direction feel right? And should the overall feel be ink on parchment (darker outlines, lighter fills) or more painted (fills are the primary element, outlines secondary)? I think the dragons should be drawn and not implied or suggested. Give me 1 number in each suit and one dragon and one flower before you do the whole set. How do I know that I'm not stealing someone's original design? Apr 5 Really good instinct to ask this. Here's how to think about it: What's Protected vs. What's Not Not protected — safe to use: The concept of mahjong tiles itself The game mechanics and tile categories The Chinese characters (萬, 中, 發, 白) — these are thousands of years old The general idea of bamboo stalks, dots, dragons on tiles Traditional mahjong tile layouts that have been in use for over a century Protected — don't copy: A specific artist's original drawing of a dragon A specific tile set's unique illustration style that someone created Commercial tile set artwork sold as a product The Safest Paths Option 1 — Commission original art Hire an illustrator on Fiverr or Upwork to draw your tile set. You own it completely. Cost: $200-500 for a full set. Option 2 — Use explicitly free/open source artwork The FluffyStuff riichi mahjong tile set on GitHub is Creative Commons licensed — free to use with attribution. It's Japanese style not American, but the bamboo and dot suits translate directly. Option 3 — I draw them for you What I create for you is original. The SVG paths I write are new artwork. The issue we've been hitting is quality at small scale — but that's solvable with patience. Option 4 — Use Unicode mahjong characters The Unicode standard includes mahjong tile characters (🀇🀈🀙🀀 etc.) — these are free, built into operating systems, and render cleanly.
